href="/wiki/Princess_Michael_of_Kent" title="Princess Michael of Kent">HRH Princess Michael of Kent</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:British_Royal_Family" title="Template:British Royal Family"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:British_Royal_Family" title="Template talk:British Royal Family"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:British_Royal_Family&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Elizabeth II</b> (Elizabeth Alexandra Mary; born 21 April 1926)<sup id="cite_ref-birthday_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birthday-2">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> is <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen of the United Kingdom">Queen of the United Kingdom</a> and the other <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">Commonwealth realms</a>. \n</p><p>Elizabeth was born in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> as the first child of the Duke and Duchess of York, later <a href="/wiki/King_George_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="King George VI">King George VI</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_The_Queen_Mother" title="Queen Elizabeth The Queen Mother">Queen Elizabeth</a>, and she was educated privately at home. Her father acceded to the throne on the <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII_abdication_crisis" title="Edward VIII abdication crisis">abdication</a> of his brother <a href="/wiki/King_Edward_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="King Edward VIII">King Edward VIII</a> in 1936, from which time she was the <a href="/wiki/Heir_presumptive" title="Heir presumptive">heir presumptive</a>. She began to undertake public duties <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_World_War_II" title="Military history of the United Kingdom during World War II">during the Second World War</a>, serving in the <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_Territorial_Service" title="Auxiliary Territorial Service">Auxiliary Territorial Service</a>. In 1947, she married <a href="/wiki/Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">Philip, Duke of Edinburgh</a>, a former prince of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, with whom she has four children: <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Charles, Prince of Wales</a>; <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Princess_Royal" title="Anne, Princess Royal">Anne, Princess Royal</a>; <a href="/wiki/Andrew,_Duke_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrew, Duke of York">Andrew, Duke of York</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Edward,_Earl_of_Wessex" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward, Earl of Wessex">Edward, Earl of Wessex</a>.\n</p><p>When her father died in February 1952, she became <a href="/wiki/Head_of_the_Commonwealth" title="Head of the Commonwealth">Head of the Commonwealth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queen_regnant" title="Queen regnant">queen regnant</a> of seven independent Commonwealth countries: the United Kingdom, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_Pakistan" title="Dominion of Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_Ceylon" title="Dominion of Ceylon">Ceylon</a>. She has reigned through major constitutional changes, such as <a href="/wiki/Devolution_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Devolution in the United Kingdom">devolution in the United Kingdom</a>, Canadian <a href="/wiki/Patriation" title="Patriation">patriation</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation_of_Africa" title="Decolonisation of Africa">decolonisation of Africa</a>. Between 1956 and 1992, the number of <a href="/wiki/States_headed_by_Elizabeth_II" title="States headed by Elizabeth II">her realms</a> varied as territories gained independence and realms, including South Africa, Pakistan, and Ceylon (renamed <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>), became republics. Her many historic visits and meetings include a <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II%27s_state_visit_to_the_Republic_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II&#39;s state visit to the Republic of Ireland">state visit to the Republic of Ireland</a> and visits to or from five popes. Significant events have included her <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II">coronation in 1953</a> and the celebrations of her <a href="/wiki/Silver_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Silver Jubilee of Elizabeth II">Silver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Golden_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Golden Jubilee of Elizabeth II">Golden</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Diamond Jubilee of Elizabeth II">Diamond Jubilees</a> in 1977, 2002, and 2012 respectively. In 2017, she became the first British monarch to reach a <a href="/wiki/Sapphire_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Sapphire Jubilee of Elizabeth II">Sapphire Jubilee</a>. She is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_monarchs_by_longevity" title="List of British monarchs by longevity">longest-lived</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_monarchs_in_Britain_by_length_of_reign" title="List of monarchs in Britain by length of reign">longest-reigning British monarch</a> as well as the world\'s <a href="/wiki/List_of_longest-reigning_monarchs" title="List of longest-reigning monarchs">longest-reigning queen regnant</a> and female <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a>, the oldest and <a href="/wiki/Current_reigning_monarchs_by_length_of_reign" class="mw-redirect" title="Current reigning monarchs by length of reign">longest-reigning current monarch</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_current_state_leaders_by_date_of_assumption_of_office" title="List of current state leaders by date of assumption of office">longest-serving current head of state</a>.\n</p><p>Elizabeth has occasionally faced <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Republicanism in the United Kingdom">republican</a> sentiments and press criticism of the <a href="/wiki/British_royal_family" title="British royal family">royal family</a>, in particular after the breakdown of her children\'s marriages, her <i><a href="/wiki/Annus_horribilis#Elizabeth_II" title="Annus horribilis">annus horribilis</a></i> in 1992, and the <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Diana,_Princess_of_Wales" title="Death of Diana, Princess of Wales">death in 1997</a> of her former daughter-in-law <a href="/wiki/Diana,_Princess_of_Wales" title="Diana, Princess of Wales">Diana, Princess of Wales</a>. However, support for the monarchy remains high, as does her personal popularity.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Elizabeth_II_1929.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Elizabeth as a thoughtful-looking toddler with curly, fair hair" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Queen_Elizabeth_II_1929.jpg/170px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_1929.jpg" width="170" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Queen_Elizabeth_II_1929.jpg/255px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_1929.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Queen_Elizabeth_II_1929.jpg/340px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_1929.jpg 2x" data-file-width="571" data-file-height="682" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Elizabeth_II_1929.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth on the <a href="/wiki/List_of_covers_of_Time_magazine_(1920s)#1929" title="List of covers of Time magazine (1920s)">cover</a> of <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)"><i>Time</i> magazine</a>, April 1929</div></div></div>\n<p>Elizabeth was born at 02:40 (<a href="/wiki/GMT" class="mw-redirect" title="GMT">GMT</a>) on 21 April 1926, during the reign of her paternal grandfather, <a href="/wiki/King_George_V" class="mw-redirect" title="King George V">King George V</a>. Her father, the Duke of York (later <a href="/wiki/King_George_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="King George VI">King George VI</a>), was the second son of the King. Her mother, the Duchess of York (later <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_The_Queen_Mother" title="Queen Elizabeth The Queen Mother">Queen Elizabeth</a>), was the youngest daughter of Scottish aristocrat the <a href="/wiki/Claude_Bowes-Lyon,_14th_Earl_of_Strathmore_and_Kinghorne" title="Claude Bowes-Lyon, 14th Earl of Strathmore and Kinghorne">Earl of Strathmore and Kinghorne</a>. She was delivered by <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">Caesarean section</a> at her maternal grandfather\'s London house: 17 Bruton Street, <a href="/wiki/Mayfair" title="Mayfair">Mayfair</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> She was <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptised</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">Archbishop of York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmo_Gordon_Lang" title="Cosmo Gordon Lang">Cosmo Gordon Lang</a>, in the private chapel of <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a> on 29 May,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-baptism_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baptism-6">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> and named Elizabeth after her mother, Alexandra after <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_of_Denmark" title="Alexandra of Denmark">George V\'s mother</a>, who had died six months earlier, and Mary after <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Teck" title="Mary of Teck">her paternal grandmother</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Called "Lilibet" by her close family,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> based on what she called herself at first,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> she was cherished by her grandfather George V, and during his serious illness in 1929 her regular visits were credited in the popular press and by later biographers with raising his spirits and aiding his recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Philip_Alexius_de_Laszlo-Princess_Elizabeth_of_York,_Currently_Queen_Elizabeth_II_of_England,1933.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Elizabeth as a rosy-cheeked young girl with blue eyes and fair hair" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Philip_Alexius_de_Laszlo-Princess_Elizabeth_of_York%2C_Currently_Queen_Elizabeth_II_of_England%2C1933.jpg/170px-Philip_Alexius_de_Laszlo-Princess_Elizabeth_of_York%2C_Currently_Queen_Elizabeth_II_of_England%2C1933.jpg" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Philip_Alexius_de_Laszlo-Princess_Elizabeth_of_York%2C_Currently_Queen_Elizabeth_II_of_England%2C1933.jpg/255px-Philip_Alexius_de_Laszlo-Princess_Elizabeth_of_York%2C_Currently_Queen_Elizabeth_II_of_England%2C1933.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Philip_Alexius_de_Laszlo-Princess_Elizabeth_of_York%2C_Currently_Queen_Elizabeth_II_of_England%2C1933.jpg/340px-Philip_Alexius_de_Laszlo-Princess_Elizabeth_of_York%2C_Currently_Queen_Elizabeth_II_of_England%2C1933.jpg 2x" data-file-width="574" data-file-height="747" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Philip_Alexius_de_Laszlo-Princess_Elizabeth_of_York,_Currently_Queen_Elizabeth_II_of_England,1933.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Philip_de_L%C3%A1szl%C3%B3" title="Philip de L\xc3\xa1szl\xc3\xb3">Philip de L\xc3\xa1szl\xc3\xb3</a>, 1933</div></div></div>\n<p>Elizabeth\'s only sibling, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Margaret" class="mw-redirect" title="Princess Margaret">Princess Margaret</a>, was born in 1930. The two princesses were educated at home under the supervision of their mother and their <a href="/wiki/Governess" title="Governess">governess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marion_Crawford" title="Marion Crawford">Marion Crawford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Lessons concentrated on history, language, literature and music.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Crawford published a biography of Elizabeth and Margaret\'s childhood years entitled <i>The Little Princesses</i> in 1950, much to the dismay of the royal family.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The book describes Elizabeth\'s love of horses and dogs, her orderliness, and her attitude of responsibility.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Others echoed such observations: <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> described Elizabeth when she was two as "a character. She has an air of authority and reflectiveness astonishing in an infant."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Her cousin <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Rhodes" title="Margaret Rhodes">Margaret Rhodes</a> described her as "a jolly little girl, but fundamentally sensible and well-behaved".<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heir_presumptive">Heir presumptive</span></h2>\n<p>During her grandfather\'s reign, Elizabeth was third in the <a href="/wiki/Line_of_succession_to_the_British_throne" class="mw-redirect" title="Line of succession to the British throne">line of succession to the throne</a>, behind her uncle <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII" title="Edward VIII">Edward, Prince of Wales</a>, and her father, the Duke of York. Although her birth generated public interest, she was not expected to become queen, as the Prince of Wales was still young. Many people believed he would marry and have children of his own.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> When her grandfather died in 1936 and her uncle succeeded as Edward VIII, she became second-in-line to the throne, after her father. Later that year, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII_abdication_crisis" title="Edward VIII abdication crisis">Edward abdicated</a>, after his proposed marriage to divorced socialite <a href="/wiki/Wallis_Simpson" title="Wallis Simpson">Wallis Simpson</a> provoked a constitutional crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, Elizabeth\'s father became king, and she became <a href="/wiki/Heir_presumptive" title="Heir presumptive">heir presumptive</a>. If her parents had had a later son, she would have lost her position as first-in-line, as her brother would have been <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a> and above her in the line of succession.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Elizabeth received private tuition in constitutional history from <a href="/wiki/Henry_Marten_(educator)" title="Henry Marten (educator)">Henry Marten</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Provosts_of_Eton_College" title="List of Provosts of Eton College">Vice-Provost</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">Eton College</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and learned French from a succession of native-speaking governesses.<sup id="cite_ref-Edu_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edu-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Girl_Guides" title="Girl Guides">Girl Guides</a> company, the <a href="/wiki/Girlguiding_London_and_South_East_England#1st_Buckingham_Palace_Company" title="Girlguiding London and South East England">1st Buckingham Palace Company</a>, was formed specifically so she could socialise with girls her own age.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Later, she was enrolled as a <a href="/wiki/Ranger_(Girl_Guide)" title="Ranger (Girl Guide)">Sea Ranger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Edu_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edu-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1939, Elizabeth\'s parents <a href="/wiki/1939_royal_tour_of_Canada" title="1939 royal tour of Canada">toured Canada</a> and the United States. As in 1927, when her parents had <a href="/wiki/Royal_visits_to_Australia#1927" title="Royal visits to Australia">toured Australia</a> and New Zealand, Elizabeth remained in Britain, since her father thought her too young to undertake public tours.<sup id="cite_ref-p54_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p54-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Elizabeth "looked tearful" as her parents departed.<sup id="cite_ref-p55_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p55-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> They corresponded regularly,<sup id="cite_ref-p55_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p55-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and she and her parents made the first royal <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_communications_cable" title="Transatlantic communications cable">transatlantic telephone</a> call on 18 May.<sup id="cite_ref-p54_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p54-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hrh_Princess_Elizabeth_in_the_Auxiliary_Territorial_Service,_April_1945_TR2832.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Hrh_Princess_Elizabeth_in_the_Auxiliary_Territorial_Service%2C_April_1945_TR2832.jpg/220px-Hrh_Princess_Elizabeth_in_the_Auxiliary_Territorial_Service%2C_April_1945_TR2832.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Hrh_Princess_Elizabeth_in_the_Auxiliary_Territorial_Service%2C_April_1945_TR2832.jpg/330px-Hrh_Princess_Elizabeth_in_the_Auxiliary_Territorial_Service%2C_April_1945_TR2832.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Hrh_Princess_Elizabeth_in_the_Auxiliary_Territorial_Service%2C_April_1945_TR2832.jpg/440px-Hrh_Princess_Elizabeth_in_the_Auxiliary_Territorial_Service%2C_April_1945_TR2832.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="588" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hrh_Princess_Elizabeth_in_the_Auxiliary_Territorial_Service,_April_1945_TR2832.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth in <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_Territorial_Service" title="Auxiliary Territorial Service">Auxiliary Territorial Service</a> uniform, <span class="nowrap">April 1945</span></div></div></div>\n<p>In September 1939, Britain entered the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, which lasted until 1945. During the war, many of London\'s <a href="/wiki/Evacuations_of_civilians_in_Britain_during_World_War_II" title="Evacuations of civilians in Britain during World War II">children were evacuated</a> to avoid the frequent <a href="/wiki/Aerial_bombing_of_cities" title="Aerial bombing of cities">aerial bombing</a>. The suggestion by senior politician <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Hogg,_1st_Viscount_Hailsham" title="Douglas Hogg, 1st Viscount Hailsham">Lord Hailsham</a><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> that the two princesses should be evacuated to Canada was rejected by Elizabeth\'s mother, who declared, "The children won\'t go without me. I won\'t leave without the King. And the King will never leave."<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Princesses Elizabeth and Margaret stayed at <a href="/wiki/Balmoral_Castle" title="Balmoral Castle">Balmoral Castle</a>, Scotland, until Christmas 1939, when they moved to <a href="/wiki/Sandringham_House" title="Sandringham House">Sandringham House</a>, Norfolk.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> From February to May 1940, they lived at <a href="/wiki/Royal_Lodge" title="Royal Lodge">Royal Lodge</a>, Windsor, until moving to <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a>, where they lived for most of the next five years.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> At Windsor, the princesses staged <a href="/wiki/Pantomime" title="Pantomime">pantomimes</a> at Christmas in aid of the Queen\'s Wool Fund, which bought yarn to knit into military garments.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In 1940, the 14-year-old Elizabeth made her first radio broadcast during the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Hour" title="Children&#39;s Hour">Children\'s Hour</a></i>, addressing other children who had been evacuated from the cities.<sup id="cite_ref-CH_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CH-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> She stated: "We are trying to do all we can to help our gallant sailors, soldiers and airmen, and we are trying, too, to bear our share of the danger and sadness of war. We know, every one of us, that in the end all will be well."<sup id="cite_ref-CH_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CH-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Special_Film_Project_186_-_Buckingham_Palace_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Special_Film_Project_186_-_Buckingham_Palace_2.jpg/220px-Special_Film_Project_186_-_Buckingham_Palace_2.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Special_Film_Project_186_-_Buckingham_Palace_2.jpg/330px-Special_Film_Project_186_-_Buckingham_Palace_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Special_Film_Project_186_-_Buckingham_Palace_2.jpg/440px-Special_Film_Project_186_-_Buckingham_Palace_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="743" data-file-height="564" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Special_Film_Project_186_-_Buckingham_Palace_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth (far left) on the balcony of <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a> with her family and <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> on 8 May 1945, <a href="/wiki/Victory_in_Europe_Day" title="Victory in Europe Day">Victory in Europe Day</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1943, Elizabeth undertook her first solo public appearance on a visit to the <a href="/wiki/Grenadier_Guards" title="Grenadier Guards">Grenadier Guards</a>, of which she had been appointed colonel the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> As she approached her 18th birthday, parliament changed the law so she could act as one of five <a href="/wiki/Counsellors_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Counsellors of State">Counsellors of State</a> in the event of her father\'s incapacity or absence abroad, such as his visit to Italy in July 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In February 1945, she was appointed as an honorary second <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_Territorial_Service#Ranks" title="Auxiliary Territorial Service">subaltern</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_Territorial_Service" title="Auxiliary Territorial Service">Auxiliary Territorial Service</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Service_number" title="Service number">service number</a> of 230873.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> She trained as a driver and mechanic and was given the rank of honorary junior commander five months later.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the end of the war in Europe, on <a href="/wiki/Victory_in_Europe_Day" title="Victory in Europe Day">Victory in Europe Day</a>, Princesses Elizabeth and Margaret mingled anonymously with the celebratory crowds in the streets of London. Elizabeth later said in a rare interview, "We asked my parents if we could go out and see for ourselves. I remember we were terrified of being recognised&#160;... I remember lines of unknown people linking arms and walking down <a href="/wiki/Whitehall" title="Whitehall">Whitehall</a>, all of us just swept along on a tide of happiness and relief."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the war, plans were drawn up to quell <a href="/wiki/Welsh_nationalism" title="Welsh nationalism">Welsh nationalism</a> by affiliating Elizabeth more closely with Wales. Proposals, such as appointing her Constable of <a href="/wiki/Caernarfon_Castle" title="Caernarfon Castle">Caernarfon Castle</a> or a patron of <a href="/wiki/Urdd_Gobaith_Cymru" title="Urdd Gobaith Cymru">Urdd Gobaith Cymru</a> (the Welsh League of Youth), were abandoned for several reasons, including fear of associating Elizabeth with <a href="/wiki/Conscientious_objector" title="Conscientious objector">conscientious objectors</a> in the Urdd at a time when Britain was at war.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Welsh politicians suggested she be made <a href="/wiki/Princess_of_Wales" title="Princess of Wales">Princess of Wales</a> on her 18th birthday. <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Morrison" title="Herbert Morrison">Herbert Morrison</a> supported the idea, but the King rejected it because he felt such a title belonged solely to the wife of a Prince of Wales and the Prince of Wales had always been the heir apparent.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In 1946, she was inducted into the Welsh <a href="/wiki/Gorsedd" title="Gorsedd">Gorsedd</a> of Bards at the <a href="/wiki/National_Eisteddfod_of_Wales" title="National Eisteddfod of Wales">National Eisteddfod of Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Princess Elizabeth went in 1947 on her first overseas tour, accompanying her parents through southern Africa. During the tour, in a broadcast to the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">British Commonwealth</a> on her 21st birthday, she made the following pledge: "I declare before you all that my whole life, whether it be long or short, shall be devoted to your service and the service of our great imperial family to which we all belong."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage">Marriage</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wedding_of_Princess_Elizabeth_and_Philip_Mountbatten" title="Wedding of Princess Elizabeth and Philip Mountbatten">Wedding of Princess Elizabeth and Philip Mountbatten</a></div>\n<p>Elizabeth met her future husband, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip_of_Greece_and_Denmark" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Philip of Greece and Denmark">Prince Philip of Greece and Denmark</a>, in 1934 and 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> They are <a href="/wiki/Cousin#Cousin_chart" title="Cousin">second cousins once removed</a> through <a href="/wiki/King_Christian_IX_of_Denmark" class="mw-redirect" title="King Christian IX of Denmark">King Christian IX of Denmark</a> and third cousins through <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>. After another meeting at the <a href="/wiki/Britannia_Royal_Naval_College" title="Britannia Royal Naval College">Royal Naval College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth,_Devon" title="Dartmouth, Devon">Dartmouth</a> in July 1939, Elizabeth \xe2\x80\x93 though only 13 years old \xe2\x80\x93 said she fell in love with Philip, and they began to exchange letters.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> She was 21 when their engagement was officially announced on 9 July 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The engagement was not without controversy; Philip had no financial standing, was foreign-born (though a British subject who had served in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> throughout the Second World War), and had sisters who had married German noblemen with <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi</a> links.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Marion Crawford wrote, "Some of the King\'s advisors did not think him good enough for her. He was a prince without a home or kingdom. Some of the papers played long and loud tunes on the string of Philip\'s foreign origin."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Later biographies reported Elizabeth\'s mother initially opposed the union, dubbing Philip "<a href="/wiki/List_of_terms_used_for_Germans#Hun" title="List of terms used for Germans">The Hun</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In later life, however, the Queen Mother told biographer <a href="/wiki/Tim_Heald" title="Tim Heald">Tim Heald</a> that Philip was "an English gentleman".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before the marriage, Philip renounced his Greek and Danish titles, officially converted from <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodoxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodoxy">Greek Orthodoxy</a> to <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>, and adopted the style <i>Lieutenant Philip Mountbatten</i>, taking <a href="/wiki/Mountbatten" class="mw-redirect" title="Mountbatten">the surname of his mother\'s British family</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Just before the wedding, he was created <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Duke of Edinburgh">Duke of Edinburgh</a> and granted the style <i>His Royal Highness</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Elizabeth and Philip were married on 20 November 1947 at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>. They received 2,500 wedding gifts from around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-news1_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news1-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Because Britain had not yet completely recovered from the devastation of the war, Elizabeth required <a href="/wiki/Rationing_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Rationing in the United Kingdom">ration coupons</a> to buy the material for <a href="/wiki/Wedding_dress_of_Princess_Elizabeth" title="Wedding dress of Princess Elizabeth">her gown</a>, which was designed by <a href="/wiki/Norman_Hartnell" title="Norman Hartnell">Norman Hartnell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In post-war Britain, it was not acceptable for the Duke of Edinburgh\'s German relations, including his three surviving sisters, to be invited to the wedding.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The Duke of Windsor, formerly King Edward VIII, was not invited either.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Elizabeth gave birth to her first child, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Charles" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Charles">Prince Charles</a>, on 14 November 1948. One month earlier, the King had issued <a href="/wiki/Letters_patent" title="Letters patent">letters patent</a> allowing her children to use the style and title of a royal prince or princess, to which they otherwise would not have been entitled as their father was no longer a royal prince.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> A second child, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Anne" class="mw-redirect" title="Princess Anne">Princess Anne</a>, was born in 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following their wedding, the couple leased <a href="/wiki/Windlesham_Moor" title="Windlesham Moor">Windlesham Moor</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a>, until July 1949,<sup id="cite_ref-news1_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news1-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> when they took up residence at <a href="/wiki/Clarence_House" title="Clarence House">Clarence House</a> in London. At various times between 1949 and 1951, the Duke of Edinburgh was stationed in the British <a href="/wiki/Crown_Colony_of_Malta" title="Crown Colony of Malta">Crown Colony of Malta</a> as a serving Royal Navy officer. He and Elizabeth lived intermittently in Malta for several months at a time in the hamlet of <a href="/wiki/Gwardaman%C4%A1a" title="Gwardaman\xc4\xa1a">Gwardaman\xc4\xa1a</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Villa_Guardamangia" title="Villa Guardamangia">Villa Guardamangia</a>, the rented home of Philip\'s uncle, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mountbatten" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Mountbatten">Lord Mountbatten</a>. The children remained in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign">Reign</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accession_and_coronation">Accession and coronation</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II">Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II_Couronnement_de_la_Reine_Elizabeth_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II_Couronnement_de_la_Reine_Elizabeth_II.jpg/170px-Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II_Couronnement_de_la_Reine_Elizabeth_II.jpg" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II_Couronnement_de_la_Reine_Elizabeth_II.jpg/255px-Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II_Couronnement_de_la_Reine_Elizabeth_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II_Couronnement_de_la_Reine_Elizabeth_II.jpg/340px-Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II_Couronnement_de_la_Reine_Elizabeth_II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="422" data-file-height="580" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II_Couronnement_de_la_Reine_Elizabeth_II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coronation of Elizabeth II, <span class="nowrap">2 June 1953</span></div></div></div>\n<p>During 1951, George VI\'s health declined, and Elizabeth frequently stood in for him at public events. When she toured Canada and visited President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> in Washington, D.C., in October 1951, her private secretary, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Charteris,_Baron_Charteris_of_Amisfield" title="Martin Charteris, Baron Charteris of Amisfield">Martin Charteris</a>, carried a draft accession declaration in case the King died while she was on tour.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In early 1952, Elizabeth and Philip set out for a tour of Australia and New Zealand by way of Kenya. On 6 February 1952, they had just returned to their Kenyan home, <a href="/wiki/Sagana_Lodge" title="Sagana Lodge">Sagana Lodge</a>, after a night spent at <a href="/wiki/Treetops_Hotel" title="Treetops Hotel">Treetops Hotel</a>, when word arrived of the death of the King and consequently Elizabeth\'s immediate <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_the_British_throne" title="Succession to the British throne">accession to the throne</a>. Philip broke the news to the new queen.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Martin Charteris asked her to choose a <a href="/wiki/Regnal_name" title="Regnal name">regnal name</a>; she chose to remain Elizabeth, "of course".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> She was <a href="/wiki/Proclamation_of_accession_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Proclamation of accession of Elizabeth II">proclaimed queen</a> throughout her realms and the royal party hastily returned to the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> She and the Duke of Edinburgh moved into <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With Elizabeth\'s accession, it seemed probable the <a href="/wiki/Royal_house" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal house">royal house</a> would bear her husband\'s name, becoming the <i>House of Mountbatten</i>, in line with the custom of a wife taking her husband\'s surname on marriage. The British Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, and Elizabeth\'s grandmother, <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Teck" title="Mary of Teck">Queen Mary</a>, favoured the retention of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Windsor" title="House of Windsor">House of Windsor</a>, and so on 9 April 1952 Elizabeth issued a declaration that <i>Windsor</i> would continue to be the name of the royal house. The Duke complained, "I am the only man in the country not allowed to give his name to his own children."<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In 1960, after the death of Queen Mary in 1953 and the resignation of Churchill in 1955, the surname <i><a href="/wiki/Mountbatten-Windsor" title="Mountbatten-Windsor">Mountbatten-Windsor</a></i> was adopted for Philip and Elizabeth\'s male-line descendants who do not carry royal titles.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Elizabeth_II_Coronation_Portrait_Herbert_James_Gunn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Queen_Elizabeth_II_Coronation_Portrait_Herbert_James_Gunn.jpg/170px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_Coronation_Portrait_Herbert_James_Gunn.jpg" width="170" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Queen_Elizabeth_II_Coronation_Portrait_Herbert_James_Gunn.jpg/255px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_Coronation_Portrait_Herbert_James_Gunn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Queen_Elizabeth_II_Coronation_Portrait_Herbert_James_Gunn.jpg/340px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_Coronation_Portrait_Herbert_James_Gunn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="925" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Elizabeth_II_Coronation_Portrait_Herbert_James_Gunn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coronation portrait of Elizabeth II by Sir <a href="/wiki/Herbert_James_Gunn" title="Herbert James Gunn">Herbert James Gunn</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Amid preparations for <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II">the coronation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Margaret,_Countess_of_Snowdon" title="Princess Margaret, Countess of Snowdon">Princess Margaret</a> told her sister she wished to marry <a href="/wiki/Peter_Townsend_(RAF_officer)" title="Peter Townsend (RAF officer)">Peter Townsend</a>, a divorc\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x9a 16 years Margaret\'s senior, with two sons from his previous marriage. The Queen asked them to wait for a year; in the words of Martin Charteris, "the Queen was naturally sympathetic towards the Princess, but I think she thought \xe2\x80\x93 she hoped \xe2\x80\x93 given time, the affair would peter out."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Senior politicians were against the match and the Church of England did not permit remarriage after divorce. If Margaret had contracted a civil marriage, she would have been expected to renounce her right of succession.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Eventually, she decided to abandon her plans with Townsend.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In 1960, she married <a href="/wiki/Antony_Armstrong-Jones,_1st_Earl_of_Snowdon" title="Antony Armstrong-Jones, 1st Earl of Snowdon">Antony Armstrong-Jones</a>, who was created <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Snowdon" title="Earl of Snowdon">Earl of Snowdon</a> the following year. They divorced in 1978; she did not remarry.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite the death of Queen Mary on 24 March, the coronation on 2 June 1953 went ahead as planned, as Mary had asked before she died.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The ceremony in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, with the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Anointing" title="Anointing">anointing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">communion</a>, was televised for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-television_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-television-73">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coronation_gown_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation gown of Queen Elizabeth II">Elizabeth\'s coronation gown</a> was embroidered on her instructions with the floral emblems of Commonwealth countries:<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> English <a href="/wiki/Tudor_rose" title="Tudor rose">Tudor rose</a>; Scots <a href="/wiki/Thistle" title="Thistle">thistle</a>; Welsh <a href="/wiki/Leek" title="Leek">leek</a>; Irish <a href="/wiki/Shamrock" title="Shamrock">shamrock</a>; Australian <a href="/wiki/Acacia_pycnantha" title="Acacia pycnantha">wattle</a>; Canadian <a href="/wiki/Maple_leaf" title="Maple leaf">maple leaf</a>; New Zealand <a href="/wiki/Cyathea_dealbata" title="Cyathea dealbata">silver fern</a>; South African <a href="/wiki/Protea" title="Protea">protea</a>; <a href="/wiki/Nelumbo_nucifera" title="Nelumbo nucifera">lotus flowers</a> for India and Ceylon; and Pakistan\'s wheat, cotton, and <a href="/wiki/Jute" title="Jute">jute</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continuing_evolution_of_the_Commonwealth">Continuing evolution of the Commonwealth</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm#From_Queen_Elizabeth&#39;s_accession" title="Commonwealth realm">Commonwealth realm \xc2\xa7&#160;From Queen Elizabeth\'s accession</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_in_February_1952.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/British_Empire_in_February_1952.png/220px-British_Empire_in_February_1952.png" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/British_Empire_in_February_1952.png/330px-British_Empire_in_February_1952.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/British_Empire_in_February_1952.png/440px-British_Empire_in_February_1952.png 2x" data-file-width="1423" data-file-height="623" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_in_February_1952.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth\'s realms (pink) and their territories and protectorates (dark red) at the beginning of her reign</div></div></div>\n<p>From Elizabeth\'s birth onwards, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> continued its transformation into the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> By the time of her accession in 1952, her role as head of multiple independent states was already established.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In 1953, the Queen and her husband embarked on a seven-month round-the-world tour, visiting 13 countries and covering more than 40,000 miles by land, sea and air.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> She became the first reigning <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Australia" title="Monarchy of Australia">monarch of Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_New_Zealand" title="Monarchy of New Zealand">New Zealand</a> to visit those nations.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> During the tour, crowds were immense; three-quarters of the population of Australia were estimated to have seen her.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Throughout her reign, the Queen has made hundreds of <a href="/wiki/List_of_state_visits_made_by_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="List of state visits made by Queen Elizabeth II">state visits</a> to other countries and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Commonwealth_visits_made_by_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Commonwealth visits made by Queen Elizabeth II">tours of the Commonwealth</a>; she is the most widely travelled head of state.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1956, the British and French prime ministers, Sir <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Eden" title="Anthony Eden">Anthony Eden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guy_Mollet" title="Guy Mollet">Guy Mollet</a>, discussed the possibility of France joining the Commonwealth. The proposal was never accepted and the following year France signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Rome" title="Treaty of Rome">Treaty of Rome</a>, which established the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a>, the precursor to the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> In November 1956, Britain and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> invaded <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">ultimately unsuccessful attempt</a> to capture the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>. Lord Mountbatten claimed the Queen was opposed to the invasion, though Eden denied it. Eden resigned two months later.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Elizabeth_II_and_the_Prime_Ministers_of_the_Commonwealth_Nations,_at_Windsor_Castle_(1960_Commonwealth_Prime_Minister%27s_Conference).jpg" class="image"><img alt="A formal group of Elizabeth in tiara and evening dress with eleven politicians in evening dress or national costume." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Queen_Elizabeth_II_and_the_Prime_Ministers_of_the_Commonwealth_Nations%2C_at_Windsor_Castle_%281960_Commonwealth_Prime_Minister%27s_Conference%29.jpg/220px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_and_the_Prime_Ministers_of_the_Commonwealth_Nations%2C_at_Windsor_Castle_%281960_Commonwealth_Prime_Minister%27s_Conference%29.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Queen_Elizabeth_II_and_the_Prime_Ministers_of_the_Commonwealth_Nations%2C_at_Windsor_Castle_%281960_Commonwealth_Prime_Minister%27s_Conference%29.jpg/330px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_and_the_Prime_Ministers_of_the_Commonwealth_Nations%2C_at_Windsor_Castle_%281960_Commonwealth_Prime_Minister%27s_Conference%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Queen_Elizabeth_II_and_the_Prime_Ministers_of_the_Commonwealth_Nations%2C_at_Windsor_Castle_%281960_Commonwealth_Prime_Minister%27s_Conference%29.jpg/440px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_and_the_Prime_Ministers_of_the_Commonwealth_Nations%2C_at_Windsor_Castle_%281960_Commonwealth_Prime_Minister%27s_Conference%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Elizabeth_II_and_the_Prime_Ministers_of_the_Commonwealth_Nations,_at_Windsor_Castle_(1960_Commonwealth_Prime_Minister%27s_Conference).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth and Commonwealth leaders at the 1960 <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Prime_Ministers%27_Conference" title="Commonwealth Prime Ministers&#39; Conference">Commonwealth Conference</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The absence of a formal mechanism within the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> for choosing a leader meant that, following Eden\'s resignation, it fell to the Queen to decide whom to <a href="/wiki/Kissing_hands" title="Kissing hands">commission to form a government</a>. Eden recommended she consult <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_5th_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 5th Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lord_President_of_the_Council" title="Lord President of the Council">Lord President of the Council</a>. Lord Salisbury and <a href="/wiki/David_Maxwell_Fyfe,_1st_Earl_of_Kilmuir" title="David Maxwell Fyfe, 1st Earl of Kilmuir">Lord Kilmuir</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a>, consulted the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">British Cabinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, and the Chairman of the backbench <a href="/wiki/1922_Committee" title="1922 Committee">1922 Committee</a>, resulting in the Queen appointing their recommended candidate: <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Suez crisis and the choice of Eden\'s successor led in 1957 to the first major personal criticism of the Queen. In a magazine, which he owned and edited,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Grigg,_2nd_Baron_Altrincham" title="John Grigg, 2nd Baron Altrincham">Lord Altrincham</a> accused her of being "out of touch".<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Altrincham was denounced by public figures and slapped by a member of the public appalled by his comments.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Six years later, in 1963, Macmillan resigned and advised the Queen to appoint <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">the Earl of Home</a> as prime minister, advice she followed.<sup id="cite_ref-r84_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r84-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The Queen again came under criticism for appointing the prime minister on the advice of a small number of ministers or a single minister.<sup id="cite_ref-r84_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r84-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In 1965, the Conservatives adopted a formal mechanism for electing a leader, thus relieving her of involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" width="38" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:\n<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II%27s_Address_to_the_United_Nations_General_Assembly" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Queen Elizabeth II&#39;s Address to the United Nations General Assembly">Queen Elizabeth II\'s Address to the United Nations General Assembly</a></b></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In 1957, she made a state visit to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, where she addressed the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> on behalf of the Commonwealth. On the same tour, she opened the <a href="/wiki/23rd_Canadian_Parliament" title="23rd Canadian Parliament">23rd Canadian Parliament</a>, becoming the first <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Canada" title="Monarchy of Canada">monarch of Canada</a> to open a parliamentary session.<sup id="cite_ref-Canada_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canada-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Two years later, solely in her capacity as Queen of Canada, she revisited the United States and toured Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-Canada_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canada-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In 1961, she toured <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, Pakistan, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> On a visit to <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> the same year, she dismissed fears for her safety, even though her host, <a href="/wiki/List_of_heads_of_state_of_Ghana" title="List of heads of state of Ghana">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah" title="Kwame Nkrumah">Kwame Nkrumah</a>, who had replaced her as head of state, was a target for assassins.<sup id="cite_ref-mac_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mac-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Harold Macmillan wrote, "The Queen has been absolutely determined all through&#160;... She is impatient of the attitude towards her to treat her as&#160;... a film star&#160;... She has indeed \'<a href="/wiki/Speech_to_the_Troops_at_Tilbury" title="Speech to the Troops at Tilbury">the heart and stomach of a man</a>\'&#160;... She loves her duty and means to be a Queen."<sup id="cite_ref-mac_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mac-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Before her tour through parts of Quebec in 1964, the press reported extremists within the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_sovereignty_movement" title="Quebec sovereignty movement">Quebec separatist movement</a> were plotting Elizabeth\'s assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> No attempt was made, but a riot did break out while she was in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>; the Queen\'s "calmness and courage in the face of the violence" was noted.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Elizabeth\'s pregnancies with Princes <a href="/wiki/Prince_Andrew,_Duke_of_York" title="Prince Andrew, Duke of York">Andrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward,_Earl_of_Wessex" title="Prince Edward, Earl of Wessex">Edward</a>, in 1959 and 1963, mark the only times she has not performed the <a href="/wiki/State_Opening_of_Parliament" title="State Opening of Parliament">State Opening of the British parliament</a> during her reign.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In addition to performing traditional ceremonies, she also instituted new practices. Her first royal walkabout, meeting ordinary members of the public, took place during a tour of Australia and New Zealand in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acceleration_of_decolonisation">Acceleration of decolonisation</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PATNIXONandQEII.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/PATNIXONandQEII.gif/170px-PATNIXONandQEII.gif" width="170" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/PATNIXONandQEII.gif/255px-PATNIXONandQEII.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/PATNIXONandQEII.gif/340px-PATNIXONandQEII.gif 2x" data-file-width="408" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PATNIXONandQEII.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Queen with <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> and American First Lady <a href="/wiki/Pat_Nixon" title="Pat Nixon">Pat Nixon</a>, 1970</div></div></div>\n<p>The 1960s and 1970s saw an acceleration in the <a href="/wiki/Decolonization" title="Decolonization">decolonisation</a> of Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>. Over 20 countries gained independence from Britain as part of a planned transition to self-government. In 1965, however, the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesian</a> Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a>, in opposition to moves towards majority rule, <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia&#39;s Unilateral Declaration of Independence">declared unilateral independence</a> from Britain while still expressing "loyalty and devotion" to Elizabeth. Although the Queen dismissed him in a formal declaration, and the international community applied sanctions against Rhodesia, his regime survived for over a decade.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> As Britain\'s ties to its former empire weakened, the British government sought entry to the European Community, a goal it achieved in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 1974, the British Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a>, advised the Queen to call a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">general election</a> in the middle of her tour of the <a href="/wiki/Austronesia" title="Austronesia">Austronesian</a> <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Rim" title="Pacific Rim">Pacific Rim</a>, requiring her to fly back to Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The election resulted in a hung parliament; Heath\'s Conservatives were not the largest party, but could stay in office if they formed a coalition with the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberals</a>. Heath only resigned when discussions on forming a coalition foundered, after which the Queen asked the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour\'s</a> <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>, to form a government.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A year later, at the height of the <a href="/wiki/1975_Australian_constitutional_crisis" title="1975 Australian constitutional crisis">1975 Australian constitutional crisis</a>, the Australian Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Gough_Whitlam" title="Gough Whitlam">Gough Whitlam</a>, was dismissed from his post by <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_Australia" title="Governor-General of Australia">Governor-General</a> Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Kerr_(governor-general)" title="John Kerr (governor-general)">John Kerr</a>, after the Opposition-controlled <a href="/wiki/Australian_Senate" title="Australian Senate">Senate</a> rejected Whitlam\'s budget proposals.<sup id="cite_ref-Aus_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aus-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> As Whitlam had a majority in the <a href="/wiki/Australian_House_of_Representatives" title="Australian House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_Australian_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the Australian House of Representatives">Speaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Scholes" title="Gordon Scholes">Gordon Scholes</a> appealed to the Queen to reverse Kerr\'s decision. She declined, saying she would not interfere in decisions reserved by the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Australia" title="Constitution of Australia">Constitution of Australia</a> for the governor-general.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The crisis fuelled <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_Australia" title="Republicanism in Australia">Australian republicanism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Aus_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aus-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silver_Jubilee">Silver Jubilee</span></h3>\n<p>In 1977, Elizabeth marked the <a href="/wiki/Silver_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Silver Jubilee of Elizabeth II">Silver Jubilee of her accession</a>. Parties and events took place throughout the Commonwealth, many coinciding with <a href="/wiki/List_of_events_during_the_Silver_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="List of events during the Silver Jubilee of Elizabeth II">her associated national and Commonwealth tours</a>. The celebrations re-affirmed the Queen\'s popularity, despite virtually coincident negative press coverage of Princess Margaret\'s separation from her husband.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> In 1978, the Queen endured a state visit to the United Kingdom by Romania\'s communist leader, <a href="/wiki/Nicolae_Ceau%C8%99escu" title="Nicolae Ceau\xc8\x99escu">Nicolae Ceau\xc8\x99escu</a>, and his wife, <a href="/wiki/Elena_Ceau%C8%99escu" title="Elena Ceau\xc8\x99escu">Elena</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> though privately she thought they had "blood on their hands".<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The following year brought two blows: one was the unmasking of <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Blunt" title="Anthony Blunt">Anthony Blunt</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Surveyor_of_the_Queen%27s_Pictures" title="Surveyor of the Queen&#39;s Pictures">Surveyor of the Queen\'s Pictures</a>, as a communist spy; the other was the assassination of her relative and in-law Lord Mountbatten by the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Paul_Joseph_James_Martin" title="Paul Joseph James Martin">Paul Martin, Sr.</a>, by the end of the 1970s the Queen was worried the Crown "had little meaning for" <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Trudeau" title="Pierre Trudeau">Pierre Trudeau</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Canada" title="Prime Minister of Canada">Canadian Prime Minister</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Post_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Post-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tony_Benn" title="Tony Benn">Tony Benn</a> said the Queen found Trudeau "rather disappointing".<sup id="cite_ref-Post_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Post-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Trudeau\'s supposed republicanism seemed to be confirmed by his antics, such as sliding down banisters at Buckingham Palace and pirouetting behind the Queen\'s back in 1977, and the removal of various <a href="/wiki/Canadian_royal_symbols" title="Canadian royal symbols">Canadian royal symbols</a> during his term of office.<sup id="cite_ref-Post_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Post-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In 1980, Canadian politicians sent to London to discuss the <a href="/wiki/Patriation" title="Patriation">patriation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Canada" title="Constitution of Canada">Canadian constitution</a> found the Queen "better informed&#160;... than any of the British politicians or bureaucrats".<sup id="cite_ref-Post_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Post-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> She was particularly interested after the failure of Bill C-60, which would have affected her role as head of state.<sup id="cite_ref-Post_109-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Post-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Patriation removed the role of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">British parliament</a> from the Canadian constitution, but the monarchy was retained. Trudeau said in his memoirs that the Queen favoured his attempt to reform the constitution and that he was impressed by "the grace she displayed in public" and "the wisdom she showed in private".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s">1980s</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElizabethIItroopingcolour_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Elizabeth in red uniform on a black horse" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ElizabethIItroopingcolour_crop.jpg/220px-ElizabethIItroopingcolour_crop.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ElizabethIItroopingcolour_crop.jpg/330px-ElizabethIItroopingcolour_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ElizabethIItroopingcolour_crop.jpg/440px-ElizabethIItroopingcolour_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2305" data-file-height="1588" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElizabethIItroopingcolour_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth riding <a href="/wiki/Burmese_(horse)" title="Burmese (horse)">Burmese</a> at the 1986 <a href="/wiki/Trooping_the_Colour" title="Trooping the Colour">Trooping the Colour</a> ceremony</div></div></div>\n<p>During the 1981 <a href="/wiki/Trooping_the_Colour" title="Trooping the Colour">Trooping the Colour</a> ceremony, six weeks before the <a href="/wiki/Wedding_of_Prince_Charles_and_Lady_Diana_Spencer" title="Wedding of Prince Charles and Lady Diana Spencer">wedding of Prince Charles and Lady Diana Spencer</a>, six shots were fired at the Queen from close range as she rode down <a href="/wiki/The_Mall,_London" title="The Mall, London">The Mall</a> on her horse, <a href="/wiki/Burmese_(horse)" title="Burmese (horse)">Burmese</a>. Police later discovered the shots were blanks. The 17-year-old assailant, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Sarjeant" title="Marcus Sarjeant">Marcus Sarjeant</a>, was sentenced to five years in prison and released after three.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The Queen\'s composure and skill in controlling her mount were widely praised.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Months later, in October, the Queen was the subject of another attack while on a visit to <a href="/wiki/Dunedin" title="Dunedin">Dunedin</a>, New Zealand. <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Security_Intelligence_Service" title="New Zealand Security Intelligence Service">New Zealand Security Intelligence Service</a> documents declassified in 2018 revealed that 17-year-old <a href="/wiki/Christopher_John_Lewis" title="Christopher John Lewis">Christopher John Lewis</a> fired a shot with a <a href="/wiki/.22_Long_Rifle" title=".22 Long Rifle">.22 rifle</a> from the fifth floor of a building overlooking the parade, but missed.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Lewis was arrested, but never charged with attempted murder or treason, and sentenced to three years in jail for unlawful possession and discharge of a firearm. Two years into his sentence, he attempted to escape a psychiatric hospital in order to assassinate <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Prince Charles</a>, who was visiting the country with <a href="/wiki/Diana,_Princess_of_Wales" title="Diana, Princess of Wales">Diana, Princess of Wales</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prince_William,_Duke_of_Cambridge" title="Prince William, Duke of Cambridge">Prince William</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From April to September 1982, the Queen was anxious but proud of her son, Prince Andrew, who was serving with British forces during the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> On 9 July, the Queen awoke in her bedroom at Buckingham Palace to find an intruder, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Fagan_incident" title="Michael Fagan incident">Michael Fagan</a>, in the room with her. In a serious lapse of security, assistance only arrived after two calls to the Palace police switchboard.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> After hosting US President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> at Windsor Castle in 1982 and visiting <a href="/wiki/Rancho_del_Cielo" title="Rancho del Cielo">his California ranch</a> in 1983, the Queen was angered when his administration ordered the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Grenada" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Grenada">invasion of Grenada</a>, one of her Caribbean realms, without informing her.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Intense media interest in the opinions and private lives of the royal family during the 1980s led to a series of sensational stories in the press, not all of which were entirely true.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_MacKenzie" title="Kelvin MacKenzie">Kelvin MacKenzie</a>, editor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)" title="The Sun (United Kingdom)">The Sun</a></i>, told his staff: "Give me a Sunday for Monday splash on the Royals. Don\'t worry if it\'s not true\xe2\x80\x94so long as there\'s not too much of a fuss about it afterwards."<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Newspaper editor <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trelford" title="Donald Trelford">Donald Trelford</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> of 21 September 1986: "The royal soap opera has now reached such a pitch of public interest that the boundary between fact and fiction has been lost sight of&#160;... it is not just that some papers don\'t check their facts or accept denials: they don\'t care if the stories are true or not." It was reported, most notably in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">The Sunday Times</a></i> of 20 July 1986, that the Queen was worried that <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>\'s economic policies fostered social divisions and was alarmed by high unemployment, <a href="/wiki/1981_England_riots" title="1981 England riots">a series of riots</a>, the violence of a <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%9385)" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984\xe2\x80\x9385)">miners\' strike</a>, and Thatcher\'s refusal to apply sanctions against the <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> regime in South Africa. The sources of the rumours included royal aide <a href="/wiki/Michael_Shea_(diplomat)" title="Michael Shea (diplomat)">Michael Shea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Secretary-General" title="Commonwealth Secretary-General">Commonwealth Secretary-General</a> <a href="/wiki/Shridath_Ramphal" title="Shridath Ramphal">Shridath Ramphal</a>, but Shea claimed his remarks were taken out of context and embellished by speculation.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher reputedly said the Queen would vote for the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(UK)" title="Social Democratic Party (UK)">Social Democratic Party</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Thatcher\'s political opponents.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher\'s biographer <a href="/wiki/John_Campbell_(biographer)" title="John Campbell (biographer)">John Campbell</a> claimed "the report was a piece of journalistic mischief-making".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Belying reports of acrimony between them, Thatcher later conveyed her personal admiration for the Queen,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> and the Queen gave two honours in her personal gift \xe2\x80\x93 membership in the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Merit" title="Order of Merit">Order of Merit</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a> \xe2\x80\x93 to Thatcher after her replacement as prime minister by <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Former Canadian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Brian_Mulroney" title="Brian Mulroney">Brian Mulroney</a> said Elizabeth was a "behind the scenes force" in ending apartheid.<sup id="cite_ref-Geddes_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geddes-125">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacQueen_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacQueen-126">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of the 1980s,  the Queen had become the target of satire.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The involvement of younger members of the royal family in the charity game show <i><a href="/wiki/It%27s_a_Royal_Knockout" class="mw-redirect" title="It&#39;s a Royal Knockout">It\'s a Royal Knockout</a></i> in 1987 was ridiculed.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In Canada, Elizabeth publicly supported politically divisive <a href="/wiki/Meech_Lake_Accord" title="Meech Lake Accord">constitutional amendments</a>, prompting criticism from opponents of the proposed changes, including <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Trudeau" title="Pierre Trudeau">Pierre Trudeau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Geddes_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geddes-125">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The same year, the elected Fijian government was deposed in <a href="/wiki/1987_Fijian_coups_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1987 Fijian coups d&#39;\xc3\xa9tat">a military coup</a>. As <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Fiji" title="Monarchy of Fiji">monarch of Fiji</a>, Elizabeth supported the attempts of the <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_Fiji" title="Governor-General of Fiji">Governor-General</a>, Ratu Sir <a href="/wiki/Penaia_Ganilau" title="Penaia Ganilau">Penaia Ganilau</a>, to assert executive power and negotiate a settlement. Coup leader <a href="/wiki/Sitiveni_Rabuka" title="Sitiveni Rabuka">Sitiveni Rabuka</a> deposed Ganilau and declared Fiji a republic.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">1990s</span></h3>\n<p>In 1991, in the wake of coalition victory in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, the Queen became the first British monarch to address a <a href="/wiki/Joint_session_of_the_United_States_Congress" title="Joint session of the United States Congress">joint meeting</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_199-1992-089-19Acropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Elizabeth, in formal dress, holds a pair of spectacles to her mouth in a thoughtful pose" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Bundesarchiv_Bild_199-1992-089-19Acropped.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_199-1992-089-19Acropped.jpg" width="170" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Bundesarchiv_Bild_199-1992-089-19Acropped.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_199-1992-089-19Acropped.jpg 1.5x,
